Stochastic differential equations
CHAPTER 4
Stochastic diï¬€erential equations
Some pertinent topics in the present chapter consist of a discussion on mar-
tingale theory, and a few relevant results on stochastic diï¬€erential equations in
spaces of ï¬nite dimension. In particular unique weak solutions to stochastic dif-
ferential equations give rise to strong Markov processes whose one-dimensional
distributions are governed by the corresponding second order parabolic type
diï¬€erential equation. Essentially speaking this chapter is part of Chapter 1 in
[146]. (The author is thankful to WSPC for the permission to include this text
also in the present book.) In this chapter we discuss weak and strong solutions
to stochastic diï¬€erential equations. We also discuss a version of the Girsanov
transformation.
1. Solutions to stochastic diï¬€erential equations
Basically, the material in this section is taken from Ikeda and Watanabe [61].
In Subsection 1.1 we begin with a discussion on strong solutions to stochastic
diï¬€erential equations, after that, in Subsection 1.2 we present a martingale
characterization of Brownian motion. We also pay some attention to (local)
exponential martingales: see Subsection 1.3. In Subsection 1.4 the notion of
weak solutions is explained. However, ï¬rst we give a deï¬nition of Brownian
motion which starts at a random position.

unique pathwise solutions, then it has unique weak solutions. We begin with a
formal deï¬nition.

4.26. Remark. The formula in (4.120) is known as the Girsanov transform
or Cameron-Martin transform of the measure P. It is a martingale measure.
Suppose that the process t ÃÃ‘ M 1ptq, as deï¬ned in (4.110) is a P-martingale.
Then the proof of Theorem 4.25 shows that the process t ÃÃ‘ Mptq, as deï¬ned

proof of Theorem 4.25 and take F â€ 1 in (4.139) to conclude that E rM 1ptqs â€œ 1
for all Ä› 0. But then the process t ÃÃ‘ M 1ptq is a P-martingale.

5.8. Definition. A complex Banach algebra pA, }Â¨}q is a complex Banach space,
endowed with a product which is compatible with the norm. The latter means
that the product pa, bq ÃÃ‘ ab, a, b in A, which is a bilinear operation, is contin-

for all a and b in A.
Examples of Banach algebras are the vector spaces C0pRÎ½q and CbpRÎ½q, equipped
with the supremum-norm and the pointwise multiplication. Let LpXq be the
vector space of all continuous linear operators on the Banach space X, supplied
with the operator norm and the composition as product. Then LpXq is a non-
commutative Banach algebra. The following theorem says that M, supplied
with the convolution product, constitutes a (complex) commutative Banach
algebra with identity Î´. Recall that M stands for the space of all complex Borel
measures on RÎ½.

5.45. Definition. A topological space E is called a Polish space if E possesses
the following properties:
(i) E is separable;
(ii) E is metrizable;
(iii) There exists a metric d on E that determines the topology and relative
to which E is complete.
Since E is metrizable property (i) is equivalent with the existence of a countable
basis for the topology.
5.46. Lemma. Let E be a Polish space.
(a) A closed subset F of E is, with the induced metric, again Polish.
(b) An open subset G of E is again Polish.
Proof. (a) The proof of assertion (a) is not diï¬ƒcult. If pUj : j P Nq is a
countable basis for the topology of E, then pUj X F : j P Nq is a countable basis
for the topology on F. Moreover, F is closed and hence it is complete with
respect to the induced metric.
(b). Let d be a metric on E, which turns E into a complete metric topological
space. Then the open subset G is Polish for the metric dG deï¬ned by
dGpx, yq â€œ dpx, yq `

5.49. Proposition. Totally order NN lexicographically. Then every closed sub-
set C of NN has a least element.
The lexicographic ordering of NN can be recursively deï¬ned. An element a â€œ

belonging to C.

every x P C, and x0 belongs to the closure of C in the metric topology introduced
in (5.54). This completes the proof of Proposition 5.49.

3. A taste of ergodic theory

Birkhoï¬€. We also indicate its relation with the strong law of large numbers.

5.74. Definition (Deï¬nitions). Let â„¦be a given set. A semi-ring is a subset
S of Ppâ„¦q, the power set of â„¦, which has the following properties:
(i) H P S;
(ii) For all A, B P S, the intersection A X B belongs to S (S is closed under
pairwise intersections);
(iii) For all A, B P S, there exist disjoint sets Ki P S, with i â€œ 1, 2, . . . , n,
such that AzB â€œ Å¤n
iâ€œ1 Ki (relative complements can be written as

A ring R is a subset of the power set of â„¦which has the following properties:
(i) H P R;
(ii) For all A, B P R, the union A Y B belongs to R (R is closed under
pairwise unions);
(iii) For all A, B P R, the relative complement AzB belongs to R (R is
closed under relative complements).
Thus any ring on â„¦is also a semi-ring.
A Boolean algebra B is deï¬ned as a subset of the power set of â„¦with the
following properties:
(i) H P B;
(ii) For all A P B and B P B the union A Y B belongs to B;
(iii) If A belongs to B, then its complement Ac â€œ â„¦zA belongs to B.
Sometimes, the following constraint is added in the measure theory context: â„¦
is the disjoint union of a countable family of sets in S.
Without proof we mention some properties. Arbitrary (possibly uncountable)
intersections of rings on â„¦are still rings on â„¦. If A is a non-empty subset of
Ppâ„¦q, then we deï¬ne the ring generated by A (noted R pAq) as the smallest
ring containing A. It is straightforward to see that the ring generated by A is
equivalent to the intersection of all rings containing A.

Some motivation is at place here. In measure theory, one is usually not inter-

by semi-rings and rings are the same, the diï¬€erence does not really matter (in
the measure theory context at least). Actually, the CarathÂ´eodoryâ€™s extension
theorem can be slightly generalized by replacing ring with semi-ring.

5.80. Example. Let E be a Ïƒ-compact topological Hausdorï¬€space, and assume

6. Stochastic processes
We begin with some deï¬nitions.
5.86. Definition. Let pâ„¦, F, Pq be a probability space, and let pE, Eq be a
locally compact Hausdorï¬€space, that satisï¬es the second countability axiom,
with Borel Ïƒ-ï¬eld E.
Often E will be chosen as R or as RÎ½.
A stochastic
process X with values in the state space E is a mapping X : r0, 8q Ë† â„¦Ã‘ E.
For every Ï‰ P â„¦the mapping t ÃÃ‘ Xpt, Ï‰q deï¬nes a path of the process. A
path is sometimes also called a realization. If we ï¬x n P N, then the mappings
Pt1,...,tn : E b Â¨ Â¨ Â¨ b E
looooomooooon

Sometimes we write Xt instead of Xptq. If n â€œ 1, then the distributions in
(5.164) are also called the marginal distributions, or marginals. However, no-
tice that a process is much more than the corresponding collection of ï¬nite-
dimensional distributions. In particular the paths or realizations of a process
are very important. For example, the continuity properties of the paths are
relevant. Often we will suppose that the paths are continuous, or that they are
continuous from the right, and possess limits from the left {c`adl`ag paths}, or

5.87. Definition. A family sub-Ïƒ-ï¬elds pFt : t Ä› 0q of F is called a ï¬ltration (or,
sometimes, also called history), if t Äƒ s implies Ft Ä‚ Fs. Thus the probability
P is deï¬ned on all Ïƒ-ï¬elds Ft.
With F8, or also F8Â´ the Ïƒ-ï¬eld generated
by Å¤
tÄ›0 Ft is meant.
If for every t Ä› 0 the equality Ft â€œ Å
sÄ…t Fs holds,
then the ï¬ltration pFt : t Ä› 0q is called continuous from the right, or right-
continuous. Let pFt : t Ä› 0q be a ï¬ltration, and put Ft` â€œ Å
sÄ…t Fs. Then the
family pFt` : t Ä› 0q is a right-continuous ï¬ltration. This ï¬ltration is called the
right closure of the ï¬ltration pFt : t Ä› 0q. A subset A of â„¦is called a P-null
set if there exists a subset A0 P F with the following properties: A Ä A0 and
PrA0s â€œ 0. Usually this is expressed by saying that A is a null set instead
of A is a P-null set. Often it is assumed that F0 contains all null sets, and
that the ï¬ltration pFt : t Ä› 0q is right-continuous. Sometimes it i said that F0
has the usual properties.
The process X is called adapted to the ï¬ltration
pFt : t Ä› 0q if for every t Ä› 0 the state variable Xptq is measurable with respect
to Ïƒ-ï¬elds Ft and E. Let Ht â€œ Ïƒ pXpuq : 0 Ä u Ä tq be the Ïƒ-ï¬eld generated
by the state variables Xpuq, 0 Ä u Ä t. The ï¬ltration pHt : t Ä› 0q is called
the internal history of the process X.
If t Ä… 0 is given, then Ht is called
the (information from the) past, Ïƒ pXptqq is called the (information from the)
present, and Ïƒ pXpuq : u Ä› tq the (information from the) future. The process X
is adapted if and only if Ht Ä Ft for every t Ä› 0.
5.88. Definition. Let X and Y be two processes. The processes X and Y are
said to be non-P-distinguishable or P-indistinguishable provided there exists a
P-null subset N with the property that for every Ï‰ R N and for every t Ä› 0
the equality Xpt, Ï‰q â€œ Y pt, Ï‰q holds. The process X is called a modiï¬cation of
the process Y (or also Y is a modiï¬cation of X) if for every t Ä› 0 there exists
a P-null set Nt with the property that Xpt, Ï‰q â€œ Y pt, Ï‰q for Ï‰ R Nt. Thus the
null set is t-dependent. If the processes X and Y are not distinguishable, then
X is a modiï¬cation of Y . In general, the converse statement is not true.
5.89. Theorem. Suppose that the process X as well as the process Y possesses
right-continuous paths. If X is a modiï¬cation of Y , then X and Y are not

8. The Doob-Meyer decomposition via Komlos theorem
Let pâ„¦, F, Pq be a probability space, let tFt : t Ä› 0u be a right continuous ï¬ltra-
tion in F and let tXptq : t Ä› 0u be a real-valued Ft-submartingale. The Doob-
Meyer decomposition theorem states that there exists an Ft-martingale tMptq :
t Ä› 0u together with an increasing predictable adapted process tAptq : t Ä› 0u,
which is right continuous P-almost surely, such that Xptq â€œ Mptq ` Aptq, t Ä› 0,
provided that the process tXptq : t Ä› 0u is of class (DL). The latter means that
for every t Ä… 0 the family tXpÏ„q : 0 Ä Ï„ Ä t, Ï„ stopping timeu is uniformly inte-
grable. Moreover this decomposition is unique in case we assume that Ap0q â€œ 0.
By Doobâ€™s optional sampling theorem every martingale is automatically of class
(DL) (see e.g. Ikeda and Watanabe [61], p.35, Ethier and Kurtz [54], p.74). An
interesting discussion of the Doob-Meyer decomposition and (sub-)martingale
theory can be found in Kopp [74]. For a nice account of the Doob-Meyer de-
composition theorem the reader may also consult van Neerven [148].
We shall employ the following result of Komlos [73]. In fact it can be interpreted
as kind of a law of large numbers.

Advanced stochastic processes: Part II
423 
Some related results
Subjects for further research and presentations
The following topics may be of interest for a presentation and/or further re-
search:
(1) Certain pseudo-diï¬€erential operators of order less than or equal to 2
can be put into correspondence with space-homogeneous or non-space-
homogeneous Markov processes. A detailed exposition can be found in
Jacob [62, 63, 64].
(2) Viscosity solutions to partial diï¬€erential equations. The standard ref-
erence for this subject is Crandall, Ishii, and Lions [35]. This topic
can also be treated in the context of Backward Stochastic Diï¬€erential
Equations (BSDEs): see, e.g., Pardoux [109].
(3) Elliptic diï¬€erential operators of second order (and Markov processes);
see, e.g., Ã˜ksendael [106].
(4) Parabolic diï¬€erential operators (of second order and Markov processes).
An interesting article in this context is Bossy and Champagnat [23].
The abstract of this paper reads: â€œWe present the main concepts of the
theory of Markov processes: transition semigroups, Feller processes, in-
ï¬nitesimal generator, Kolmogorovâ€™s backward and forward equations,
and Feller diï¬€usion.
We also give several classical examples includ-
ing stochastic diï¬€erential equations (SDEs) and backward stochastic
diï¬€erential equations (BSDEs) and describe the links between Markov
processes and parabolic partial diï¬€erential equations (PDEs). In par-
ticular, we state the Feynman-Kac formula for linear PDEs and BSDEs,
and we give some examples of the correspondence between stochastic
control problems and Hamilton-Jacobi-Bellman (HJB) equations and
between optimal stopping problems and variational inequalities. Sev-
eral examples of ï¬nancial applications are given to illustrate each of
these results, including European options, Asian options, and Ameri-
can put options.â€
(5) Solutions to stochastic diï¬€erential equations and the corresponding sec-
ond order diï¬€erential equation (of parabolic type) satisï¬ed by the one-
dimensional distributions.
(6) Backward stochastic diï¬€erential equations and their viscosity solutions;
see, e.g. Pardoux [109], Van Casteren [147], Boufoussi and Van Cast-
eren [24, 25], Boufoussi, Van Casteren and Mhardy [26].
(7) Heat equation on a Riemannian manifold.
A relevant book in this
context is [59]. For connections with stochastic diï¬€erential equations
on manifolds see, e.g., Elworthy [52, 53].
(8) Oscillatory integrals and related path integrals. There is a lot of lit-
erature on this subject. Nice papers on this topic are Albeverio and
Mazzucchi [1, 2]. Interesting books are, e.g., Mazzucchi [95], Johnson
and Lapidus [65], and Kleinert [69].
(9) Malliavin calculus, or stochastic calculus of variations, and applica-
tions to regularity properties of integral kernels. For details see e.g.
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Advanced stochastic processes: Part II
424 
Some related results
Nualart [103, 104]. Other references which contain results on and ap-
plications of Malliavin calculus include: Cruzeiro and Malliavin [36],
Stroock [127, 128, 129], Cruzeiro and Zambrini [37], [38]. Of course
the original work by Malliavin should not be forgotten: [92]. The book
by Bismut [18] combines Malliavin calculus with the theory of large
deviations. For a discussion on Malliavin calculus in relation to LÂ´evy
processes see, e.g., Osswald [108]. A rather elementary approach to
Malliavin calculus can be found in Friz [56]. For application to sto-
chastic diï¬€erential equations see, e.g., Takeuchi [135]. For applications
of Malliavin calculus to operator semigroups see, e.g., LÂ´eandre [83, 84].
For Malliavin calculus without probability theory see [82].
(10) Books and papers with literature on ï¬nancial mathematics include:
LeÂ´on, SolÂ´e, Utzet, and Vives [85], Nualart and Schoutens [105], Malli-
avin and Thalmaier [93], Karatsas and Shreve [66], Gulisashvili [60],
El Karoui and Mazliak [51], El Karoui, Pardoux and Quenez [49], Lim
[87].
Other references include Zhang and Zhou (editors) [155] and
Tsoi, Nualart and Yin [138].
(11) Another interesting subject is â€œErgodic theoryâ€ and, correspondingly,
invariant measures. We mention some references: Krengel [75], Karlin
and Taylor [67], Meyn and Tweedie [97], Eisner and Nagel [48], Van
Casteren [146], Seidler [119], Goldys [58], [115].
(12) Central limit theorems and related results are also relevant.
Again
we mention some references: Bhattaraya and Waymire [15], Nourdin
and Peccati [101], Barbour and Chen [8], Berckmoes, Lowen and Van
Casteren [11, 12, 13, 14], Tao [137], Stein [124, 125], Chen, Goldstein
and Shao [31], Barbour and Hall [9].
(13) Investigate Markov processes with a Polish space as state space: see,
e.g., Sharpe [120], Swart and Winter [134], Van Casteren [146], Bovier
[27].
(14) Discuss and make a careful study of the Skorohod space as described
in Remark 3.40. Try to include applications to convergence properties
of stochastic processes.
(15) Discuss stochastic analysis in the inï¬nite-dimensional context. A nice
and relevant survey paper is [150] written by van Neerven, Veraar and
Weis. A simpliï¬ed version in Dutch is authored by van Neerven: see
[149].
